## Transcript

- [00:00] People have been having sex and writing songs about it and carving statues of it and changing
- [00:05] fashion for it since the dawn of humanity, but it wasn't until fairly recently in the
- [00:10] 1940s that serious Western scientific study of sex began.
- [00:13] And by most accounts you can thank one guy for that: Alfred Kinsey.
- [00:18] Kinsey was born in 1894 in New Jersey, to poor strict Methodist parents.
- [00:23] He studied etymology in college, graduated with degrees in biology and psychology before
- [00:27] heading to Harvard.
- [00:28] His doctoral thesis was on the genealogy of Gall wasps, a subject he tackled with intensity;
- [00:34] meticulously collecting over five million samples, measuring hundreds of thousands of
- [00:39] specimens.
- [00:40] And wasps are interesting and everything, but Kinsey's interests drifted toward human
- [00:43] sexuality and his research in Indiana University paved the way for decades of study into sex.
- [00:49] He surveyed thousands of men and women about their sexual habits and histories, and found
- [00:53] all sorts of interesting things related to sexual preferences, masturbation, orgasms,
- [00:58] and pre-marital sex.
- [00:59] He established the "Kinsey scale" indicating degrees of sexual orientation) and went on
- [01:03] to write the seminal texts on sexual behavior in the human male and female respectively.
- [01:09] Like many researchers of human behavior, Kinsey and his colleagues were - and in some ways
- [01:13] still are - pretty controversial.
- [01:15] But he definitely succeeded in two very important tasks.
- [01:18] One: he was an early adopter of a scientific approach to studying sex, and two: he showed
- [01:23] that the popular perception of what people do and don't do sexually, versus what people
- [01:28] actually do and feel, was often very different.
- [01:32] Since then we've seen innumerable sex-related studies examining the physiological and psychological
- [01:36] and social elements of sex.
- [01:38] And, there's a whole lot of lingering misinformation and judging morality swirling around the subject
- [01:43] of sex.
- [01:44] It can be a tricky topic for sure but we are here to clear some things up.
- [01:48] It's the entire purpose of life so there's no reason to blush.
- [01:58] So.
- [02:01] Sex.
- [02:04] That one little word has complicated so many lives.
- [02:08] The desire for or lack of sex has spawned poetry and made babies and transmitted diseases
- [02:12] and cost money and driven people batty and kept late-night cable in business.
- [02:17] Even the word itself can mean many different things.
- [02:20] First we've got the verb kind of sex, the physical process of engaging in sexual acts
- [02:24] and intercourse, which, probably I don't need to describe to you.
- [02:27] Then we've got the biological definition, having to do with the anatomical parts that
- [02:31] go with the designations of male or female or intersex - those who were born with the
- [02:36] reproductive parts that don't fall into the predominant definitions of male or female.
- [02:39] And these are quite separate from gender, or an individual's sense of identifying as

- [02:48] actual reproductive plumbing.
- [02:50] For transgender people, for instance, gender identity typically doesn't match biological
- [02:54] sex.
- [02:55] And remember that gender identity is completely different from sexual orientation, which we'll
- [02:59] talk about in a minute.
- [03:00] So beyond definitions, we have the physiological and psychological aspects of sex.
- [03:05] Let's start with the physiological, and with that, Masters and Johnson.
- [03:09] In the late 1950's and 60's, American gynecologists Williams Masters, and his collaborator and
- [03:13] future wife, sexologist Virginia Johnson, did something no researchers had ever done
- [03:18] before.
- [03:19] They invited nearly 700 male and female volunteers, many of them sex workers, to come into their
- [03:24] lab and get it on, either alone or with their partners.
- [03:28] Their aim?
- [03:29] To record the body's physiological response to sex.
- [03:32] This involved wearing a whole lot of wires and heart monitors and such, and is probably
- [03:36] about as sexy as it sounds.
- [03:38] All the volunteers had to be willing and able to show arousal and be capable of orgasm.
- [03:42] And over the years, Masters and Johnson recorded more than ten thousand "sexual cycles".
- [03:47] The main thing they documented was that a complete sexual response cycle involved four
- [03:52] distinct stages - excitement, plateau, orgasm, and resolution - which Masters and Johnson
- [03:58] maintained happened in a linear way, one after the other.
- [04:02] In the excitement phase, things are gettin' goin', blood is rushing to all the necessary
- [04:06] places, genital areas are becoming engorged and secreting lubricant.
- [04:10] Next comes the plateau phase.
- [04:11] Pulse, blood pressure, and breathing rates keep increasing and genitals are becoming
- [04:15] fully engorged.
- [04:16] The penis is often secreting pre-ejaculate as vaginal secretions increase until the big
- [04:22] event orgasm during which muscles all over the body contract and breathing and pulse
- [04:27] rates hit their peak.
- [04:28] Of course, a biological male orgasm typically releases sperm that may lead to fertilization,
- [04:32] depending on the situation, but female arousal and orgasm also help facilitate conception,
- [04:38] again depending on the situation as those muscle contractions and lubrication help draw
- [04:42] up and retain sperm in the uterus.
- [04:44] Finally, the body comes back down to its normal state of affairs during the resolution phase.
- [04:48] It's during this phase that biosex males enter a refractory period during which they're unable
- [04:53] to orgasm again for a few minutes to a day or more whereas biological females refractory
- [04:58] period is very short in comparison.
- [05:00] While the four stage model of sexual response is still taught today, some have criticized
- [05:04] both its rigid linear setup, arguing that things don't always work so tidily in the
- [05:09] bedroom, and it's insistence in including orgasm which doesn't happen for everyone all
- [05:13] the time.
- [05:14] Others also question the model's clinical focus on only physiological factors arguing
- [05:18] the cultural attitudes, psychological and relationship factors, and other external details
- [05:23] should also be considered when looking at sexual response.
- [05:26] I'll get back to that in a minute, but before we move on to the psychology of sex, we got
- [05:30] to talk about hormones.
- [05:31] You remember hormones, those chemical messengers brewed up by the endocrine system that travel
- [05:36] through the bloodstream and regulate all sorts of physiological and behavioral activities
- [05:40] from growth to digestion to sleep to sex.
- [05:43] Our sex hormones serve two major purposes.
- [05:45] One, they direct the physical development of biological sex characteristics, and two,
- [05:50] they help activate sexual behavior.
- [05:53] Estrogens like estradiol contribute to female sex characteristics and are secreted in greater
- [05:57] amounts by females than males and while all humans make testosterone, it's the predominate
- [06:01] sex hormone for males, stimulating the growth and development of male sex characteristics.
- [06:06] Now most female mammals become sexually receptive when their estrogens peak during ovulation,
- [06:11] but it doesn't really work that way for humans.
- [06:14] Our hormones are more loosely related to sexual behavior, although studies have found that
- [06:18] in general female's sexual desire spikes slightly around ovulation, when woman are most fertile
- [06:23] and males can also be affected by this spike, responding with higher levels of testosterone
- [06:28] when ovulating women are around.
- [06:30] But these short term changes hardly compare to the larger more major hormonal shifts that
- [06:35] occur throughout a life time.
- [06:37] Puberty, for one, tends to get everyone a lot more hot and bothered and interested in
- [06:41] dating and gazing at posters of their favorite celebrity crushes and later in life, as sex
- [06:46] hormone production normally decreases, our amorous urges and endeavors tend to decrease
- [06:51] as well.
- [06:52] Age affects our libido.
- [06:53] But in the end you might think of sex hormones as fuel for your sexual engine, and while
- [06:58] an engine can't run on a totally empty tank, it also won't run any better or worse on a
- [07:03] full tank versus a half tank.
- [07:05] We need our sex hormones, but we also need the right psychological stimuli to turn us
- [07:10] on and keep us going sexually.
- [07:13] So, finally, let's look at some of these psychological aspects of sex.
- [07:17] First, there are the very important social and cultural influences.
- [07:21] Things like your families, your societies, your religions, and your personal values.
- [07:25] Does your community view sex merely as the means for reproduction or can it be fun too?
- [07:30] What are the views on premarital sex, and homosexuality, showing some skin or kissing
- [07:34] in public?
- [07:35] Then there are the influences of external stimuli.
- [07:37] In western society, we're constantly bombarded with sexually charged content from movies
- [07:42] and TV to advertisements, R&B slow jams, and Victoria Secret catalogs.
- [07:47] And constantly looking at images of things that you find extremely attractive can lead
- [07:51] to folks viewing more average people, even their own partners, as being less attractive.
- [07:56] But our sexual desire is also fueled by internal stimuli.
- [07:59] Our imagination and memories and fantasies.
- [08:02] According to plenty of studies, at least 95% of people fantasize about sex at some point.
- [08:06] The thing you need to keep in mind is that none of these factors work independently of
- [08:09] each other.
- [08:10] How we respond to both external and internal stimuli can be really heavily influenced by

- [09:50] born, there are no clear environmental factors that influence your sexual orientation.

- [09:59] like genetics, brain anatomy, prenatal conditions, or other things.

- [10:13] isn't choice, but rather a naturally varying occurrence among human beings, like height.
